      \n

      Traveller. Played Adam Dray\u2019s \u201cFree Trader\u201d scenario, which is all about finding and putting a ship into operation. Needless to say, we never even really found a ship. But what a blast! Great table of players meant even our failures were entertaining.
      \nThat was "Merchant of Dennis."

      Glad you enjoyed it! The group in the other session fared slightly better than you guys, and your character was the Marquis with a huge pocket full of money (and every single character they chose at the other table was flat-out broke).

      I had fun running this.

      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n At DEXcon, I ran Mongoose Traveller (1e)...

      "Merchant of Dennis," which focuses on the logistical steps that usually get handwaved between "I muster out of the military" and "we ply the starlanes in a free trader starship." The PCs get discharged out of the Navy for gross incompetence and unceremoniously dumped on a "nicely inconspicuous" planet named Dennis with no cash. Then they pursue their Big Dream of controlling their own destiny as free traders. This involves combing junkyards for hulks that can be fixed up, securing a loan through banks or sharks, fixing up the ship, and navigating organized crime and bureaucracy along the way.

      I ran that one twice. The first table (with Mel White) had lots of money and options and made a lot of missteps and just had some really bad dice luck. They were hella entertaining, though! Invite everyone important on the planet to a party and watch the fireworks. The second table started with literally zero money, spent the first hour just trying to find food and shelter, and worked up a scheme to illegally launder ship shares through the black market with a mob boss as a silent partner.

      Independence 2776, is where I transplant the American Revolution into an alternate Traveller universe and file off the real world references. Specifically, it's the bit where Knox convinces Washington to let him take a bunch of patriots off to steal cannons abandoned at Ft. Ticonderoga to help with the Boston Harbor blockade. All transformed into science fiction with hyperspace and meson cannons and you just have to have a Lafayette character even if he doesn't really belong on that mission.

      I ran that one three times. The first was sketchy as I was really nervous, underprepared, and some of the pregen stuff just didn't work right. My wife helped me rejigger the character backgrounds a little and I moved some skills around so that everyone felt useful in every part of the story. The second and third games were amazing.

      The final game was a family of four players: Mom and Dad, and two children, ages 9 and 11. Once I got the parents to stop helicoptering (I think they were concerned that the kids weren't "playing right" and I'd be upset), the kids (and parents) performed marvelously. I softpedaled the androids-as-SF-slaves aspects of the setting quite a bit, but my heart melted when the 11-year-old girl playing the emancipated android character, "TK-44," asked me and her mom, "Why does my character not have a real name?" and Mom told her, "Do you want to have a real name? What do you want it to be?" Also when the 9-year-old boy, playing the "alien" local / Native, got bullied in a human bar and eventually was challenged to a duel. The young player insistently de-escalated the fight and eventually walked out of the bar, honor be damned, because he wanted to live in a world where there was less fighting. Of course, in the final space battle scene, he had no problem pointing out that he should be able to use the grenade launcher (that I put on his sheet and forgot about) to attack the space fighter ships that were harassing them, and cackling with glee when he rolled two "hull" hits in the same location, meaning that he shot through the hole he had made and exploded the fuel tank inside.

      \n

      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I had a great weekend and played a number of games (but most were board games). On Friday, I got to try Mobile Frame Zero: Firebrands. There were three of us, and we surprisingly quickly had an engaging situation and characters that we cared about.

      I really enjoyed how Solitaire (which you run at the end of character creation) gives you backstory, what you're up to, and what you're striving for, plus a challenge to work that into conversation. I was the insurgent, who had scouted out a soft caravan... and the game started with me chasing one of those offworlders!

      It was a good time and played out quickly--about 2.5 hours--and felt like a solid chapter from a book.

      [Among the skipped over board games, I got to try leaving earth for the first time. It was interesting; lots of simple computation for a space race that felt super competitive and glorious.]

      \n\n2097 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nCurse of Strahd, third night this week. TPK.
      Later I looked and thought "why was it so hard, what did we miss?"
      And the players were like "no, move on, accept the TPK, we'll make new characters".
      But I was like.. why was it so difficult? And I saw something big that I completely missed as DM. It says, and paraphrasing to avoid spoilers, "It is so noisy so it's easy to sneak there".
      So I told the assassin PC to replay that initial few rounds [he had been blasted away in one round in the original playthrough]. And sure enough, he could drop in 2d8+10d6 damage with his silvered sword. 38 damage.
      And, since when the last PC went down, their enemy had 34 damage, I wanted them to then be alive.
      But they were so reluctant. They were like "Sandra. These things happen. We'll make new characters, don't worry." And then they offered a compromise: let's roll off death saves together to see who wakes up first, the last PC who fell, and the enemy.
      And... they made their death saves at the same time. So 1d4 hours roll off!
      And I rolled a one and I was happy because I misthought and thought low = bad and that he just had to roll over. But no. He reminded me that he had to roll higher. And I started spinning around in the room, I was so upset.
      And they were like "no, relax, don't make our characters survive. Move on.

      Removing the high stakes from D&D and all you've got left is yahtzee."

      And, and, and... the lingering injury the enemy sustained will be lethal in as many days as the enemy had HP unless someone can tend to the wound and make a DC 15 roll. But who will save this enemy? If anyone?
      \n

      \n A one-off Roll for Shoes with conch-passing. Some of the more memorable skills from the session were Aikido 2, Dropkicking 3, Gravitas 4, Screaming 2, and Shounen Physics 2. We also encountered "Macho Cashier" Randy Savage.\n
      Parcel out the dice among all the players and have them roll. The total is the number of adult rabbits lost or killed in the great trek. At least one rabbit per die should be a named rabbit.       \nAmazing.

      A one-off Roll for Shoes
      \nThat reminds me, in the second Night Witches scenario I ran, the airwomen of the 588th had a party to attend--and uniform regs had been relaxed, at least for them, so there was a scramble get civvies. A section of the 3rd Squadron had a crate of shoes, so one PC went scrounging for something to wear...

      So yeah. We were rolling for shoes

      \n\norklord \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Lady Blackbird We had a gender-flipped Captain Cyra Vance, a Lady Blackbird who was shocked, then interested, then intrigued by the idea of what Cyra's attraction to her meant (despite someone pointing out that many of Cornwallis University's students--the Lady went there--were, um, "sapphic until graduation".) Kale's player interpreted the Key of the Mission as meaning he had been hired by Flint to make sure the Lady got to her destination (and this was a pretty dark Kale).

      I usually try to give my players the chance to interact with Captain Hollas (there are so few named NPCs that I assume that Hollas must be important), and as usually happens in those cases, they kidnapped the good Captain. I had great fun sowing discord among the PCs, surprised everyone by having Hollas give his word of honor to protect Lady Blackbird (he knew she was a noblewoman, and once he gave his word he stopped speculating on her identity), and finally had him do a heel-face turn when he confirmed that Vance had abandoned the cruiser Hand of Glory because she wouldn't fire on a refugee ship--the loss of the Glory was revenge taken by the rebels after they pressed the attack (Vance was unable to convince the other captain to break off.)

      It was one of the better Lady Blackbirds I've done :-)
      \nOh, that's clever. I usually have Hollas up in the bridge doing cutscenes of him reacting to his ship blowing up. That's much cooler!

      Also, I've seen gender-swapping Kale a few times, and even a female Snargle, but never had a female Vance. That's keen.

      \n As we were having an off-week with partial attendance with my regular D&D 5e group, I had a chance to run Gangbusters for the folks who were there.

      We ran the demo adventure that came with the original boxed set as a way to learn the rules, then did a bit of more open-form gaming with the players rolling up a starting criminal intent on getting rich or dying trying.

      Hopefully, once the other two players are back, we can either do some more criminality, or roll secondary characters for the game's few modules that were published and play a bit more on the Law Enforcement side of things.

      \n\n2097 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nDead of Winter, three diegetic missions in one extradiegetic day.
      I only won the second of three. I voted to exile a person and I got everyone against me (I had the knife so I could force the issue). In the end, that won us the game because he was indeed the traitor (I was the "germophobe" [mysophobe] so I just had to keep my team, Sophie and Loretta, clean, which I could do]. But, the traitor misunderstood his exiled card so he helped us with the main objective which he thought he had to do.
      So it was a bit of an ill-gotten gain. One other player won beside me but the last colonist forgot about his secret card. Obv we haven't understood the game yet.

      I actually, hmm, not sure if I hate or love the game.
      It's a logistics game more than an "in character" game.
      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n So, I played my Julie d'Aubigny analog in our ongoing Pathfinder game yesterday. She's a chaotic good ifrit bard 6 / swashbuckler 3 named Julia Darby. She kills monsters with her voice, and sometimes with her flaming rapier +1, flameburst. We've played since 1st level.

      We're underground. Julia doesn't like it underground at all, but, like, the world is seriously in danger here. An elder god was trapped in these caves a long time ago and he seems to be trying to get out. We figured out that he devours a small part of a creature and then has some control over that creature forever. He's taken fingers and limbs from a giant horde of hobgoblins and they're threatening to spill over into our local kingdom. That's what got us down here.

      After defeating one of his abberation minions, he showed up. When his spell resistance totally defeated my Sow Thoughts spell attempt (and I had rolled an 18 to break his spell resistance), I realized that we had very little chance of defeating this thing in combat. I managed to negotiate the release of our halfling ninja's mom, who was (we thought) captured by the godling, and the bigbad let us leave, as long as we went into the lower passages instead of back the way we came.

      Sure, we suspected it was a trap and stuff. We were ready for a fight. What we got instead was a hazardous environment. The caves on the way into the depths have two problems:

      1. There are runes on the caves that channel arcane energy. Cast an arcane spell and bad shit can happen. I got Confused when I cast a spell at one point.

      2. There's some kind of poison effect due to a sort of visual hallucination (but not illusion?) that requires Fortitude saves to avoid taking a 1-point Constitution drain (permanent loss until Restored). We make those checks once per hour and the DC is 13.

      We had to travel for hours and hours. Julia's Constitution dropped from 12 to about 8. We eventually started facing Exhaustion for not sleeping, because we didn't want to spend 6 hours in one place.

      Sifting through everything on my character sheet, I eventually came across this option.

      I have a Magic Vestment that lets me cast spells off scrolls without losing them. I have a few large scrolls with a sort of library of 1st-3rd level bard spells. One of those spells is Mirror Hideaway, which lets turns a large enough mirror into a portal that eight people can crawl into, safe from magical effects outside, for six hours. Our party is six or so plus three hobgoblins who are accompanying us. At first, I thought we could use my pocket mirror, and have the ranger pick it up and run with us in it, but 1) we can't enter a mirror so small and 2) you can't move it very far without the spell ending.

      Eventually, we had the cleric Create Water on the shiny black rock, and we used it as a mirror for the spell. The characters with the best Fortitude saves stayed outside while we caught six hours of sleep in the mirror and recharged our spells and powers. The cleric is gonna pray for a whole lot of Slow Poison spells to help us get through the rest of this leg of the caves.

      We're not sure how much further we have to go, but we can use this strategy once a day.

      One thing resource-management games can do well, that story-oriented games without resource management don't do so well, is make you feel really desperate and afraid. We're running out of spells and gadgets. We're running out of options. Hell, we're running out of Constitution (thus life). Do we turn around and go back and fight an overwhelming foe while we're in a terribly weakened state? Do we continue into the uncertain depths? Will we die down here because we thought it would be the safer path?

      \n

      \n
      \nSagas of the Icelanders which was my chance to alpha-test the new Man playbook I threw together and was well-received on G+, at least (many thanks to my Gothi, Jason Morningstar, for heroic layout.) The playbook worked fine, and we had a pretty decent session, although some of my players who haven't been GMed by me in a while were probably not quite ready for the deep plunge that is SotI. (As usual, there were a few rough edges; I think it may be because SotI makes the MC step up and make pretty hard moves with less mechanical padding.) Ambushes, attempted thrall escapes, and a massacre in a longhouse--so, pretty typical for Sagas.

      Fall of Magic: I have to justify the purchase by playing it a lot, is what I told people. That shouldn't be a problem based on these results; we had a truly lovely game as Vago the Stained Glass golem (who had no mind but reflected the will of the people of the Realm), Justice the...raven person? (Shapeshifter, for sure), and Ellamura the Midwife found themselves on the way to the Umbra. When we were done, we all just turned to each other and said "...wow."

      \n
      Sagas of the Icelanders which was my chance to alpha-test the new Man playbook I threw together and was well-received on G+, at least (many thanks to my Gothi, Jason Morningstar, for heroic layout.) The playbook worked fine, and we had a pretty decent session, although some of my players who haven't been GMed by me in a while were probably not quite ready for the deep plunge that is SotI. (As usual, there were a few rough edges; I think it may be because SotI makes the MC step up and make pretty hard moves with less mechanical padding.) Ambushes, attempted thrall escapes, and a massacre in a longhouse--so, pretty typical for Sagas.
      \nThrall escapes? Where do they plan to escape to?
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nAviatrix \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Thrall escapes? Where do they plan to escape to?
      \nThorolf, being an outlaw, had an obligation to leave Iceland and had found a Danish sea captain--my sea captains always seem to be Danes--who was willing to take them to the Orkneys, where perhaps Osk and our other thrall Ref would be able to find a way back to Eire.

      Sadly, Thorolf, being an outlaw, was kind of a jerk and his original plan was to sell the thralls (even though he didn't own them) to the Dane. At the last moment he was seized by remorse and told the two thralls the plan. The Dane offered to ferry them anyway, but it was clear he was going to do Bad Things to them.*

      This was a bit of a subpar development on my side, but this was a one-shot and we had maybe an hour left to play, so I got cold feet about separating everyone. (Into the bank of "Trix's bad GMing decisions"; we have to open up another vault soon, there's no room left in this one.)

      *Additionally, my Danish sea captains all tend to be jerks. My apologies to all Danes, sea-captain or no.

      \n
      \n I did get to play In A Wicked Age and it went well. It was a good group of players at a very small meetup (only 4 this month, way down from our ~10 average). We got up and running and created a decent story in only a few hours.

      Since this one went so well (we avoided gonzo experimentation), I'd be interested in seeing what'd come next in a sequel. Backtracking: I say it went well, and we did get three interesting characters out of it, but. The but was the goal/direction overlap; I started with a meeting, but there weren't strong cross purposes between the characters I chose. Of the three players, the outland merchant kind of bounced into an interesting semi-parallel game after that first scene; he was busy extracting his revenge on NPCs who formed a separate social circle from those the other two characters wrestled with.

      \n\nmilesgaborit \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Oh wow...it was a big week...

      So, I started the week with a big camping trip with a bunch of other families: 20-odd kids, nearly 40 people total. The overlap with this group of kids and the weekly D&D group is pretty significant, so a lot of gaming happened in the woods. Board games included Coup: Reformation; Ticket to Ride; Give Me the Brain; Dragon Slayer; and Lunch Money. And we also played a little freeform LARP scenario I made for the occasion - It Came from The Lake! - which was about fungus monsters. Good times!

      And THEN! I got to attend GoPlayNW last weekend, which is always a highlight of my gaming year. Here's what I played there:

      Icons - the GM ran this game with a stripped down "let's just story game this!" ruleset for Icons. The setup was a riff on the old X-Men Inferno storyline, but instead of the villain trying to destroy the world to get revenge on ONE of her old exes, ALL of the heroes had actually dated (and somehow spurned) the supervillain. We had some really cool moments (and I LOVED my super-earnest-but-with-zero-emotional-intelligence sentient AI hero - his was the saddest story of all!), but the Icons system (and chargen in particular) still got in the way some and slowed things down. Still, big fun!

      Night Witches - This was my first game of Night Witches and it did not disappoint! NW is probably the crunchiest version of PbtA I've encountered - there just seem to be a lot of moving parts - but everything slots together so elegantly. I'm really in love with the Marks subsystem - it's a nice way of mechanically codifying "story" damage, and gives another cool output for Moves with negative consequences. Going to be stealing from it heavily

      Tall Pines - This is my game, which is basically a Twin Peaks emulator - a surreal murder mystery story making machine. Played a great game of it on Saturday night, and got good feedback. I've got one more new wrinkle I want to playtest before I call this thing finished and ready for public consumption. Our story involved a rich older woman who had ritualistically killed her much younger lover...with the assistance of a travelling salesman's ghost. Or something. There was also a hole in the woods that seemed to connect to another dimension where the murder victim was still alive, but as some sort of animal maybe. Most of the big reveals came in the form of taped hypnotist sessions with the victim before their death. It was a successfully weird game!

      Improv for LARPers Workshop - Karen Twelves ran a sequel to her much loved Improv for Gamers workshop. Got to do some fun improvisational work with some of my favorite people - and also new friends! As usual, came away with a ton of insights that I will do my best to bring forward into my next game.

      That was GoPlay. And then on Tuesday my biweekly D&D group played a session in our ongoing Curse of Strahd game. The GM gave us an excuse to split the party and we took the bait. Fortunately for us (and unfortunately for the GMs prep), our mini-parties of 8th level characters made short work of every encounter we faced. I even one-shotted a leveled NPC Druid. 8th level storm clerics are not to be effed with.
      \n

      \n Hey, I ran City of Brass, my "West Marxist" D&D 5e sandbox again on Wednesday. Third installment. The procedural elements are working well: rolling random social problems, writing them on cards, and awarding XP if they resolve them partially or fully, and making the problems worse if they come up again on the 2d12 roll or if they're unresolved.

      \nTenra Bansho Zero. Our first session of a campaign. We haven't played TBZ before except for a one-shot once. I'm playing a princess, last of the thought-destroyed line, raised on a farm, just found out, etc. Other PCs are two companions sworn to the clan.

      Didn't go very well... session was slow, no fights, some fun roleplay, but overall disjointed, halting, and weak. Not sure what the root cause was. I proposed "There's Got To Be A Boss Fight" (http://www.tenra-rpg.com/blog/?p=49), GM thought it was more that his scenario was derailed when we decided to flee the village rather than stay for the narrated princess-killer to show up, and he didn't react nimbly enough, and then we kept trying to flee (what we thought was) overwhelming forces.

      \n Last Sunday:

      \u2014 finished our 3-players game of Spione. Good game and great ending, mixing personal tragedy with a humorous-but-scary, almost Kafkaesque portrayal of bureaucracy, finishing with an intensely poetic vignette. Just a few minor hiccups, in the form of small procedural details that we couldn't find instructions for in the text.

      \u2014 that left us with plenty of time for another game, so we chose to try Firebrands. It was interesting, although we found that in a 3-player game our opportunities for developing the narrative were too constrained by taking turns picking from the available "games", as there is virtually no direct interaction with any NPCs, so that main characters can only be developed through interactions with other main characters. I understand this is entirely by design, so I expect the game to run smoother with a larger number of players, which means a larger pool of characters to interact with.

      Earlier today:

      \u2014 got to play Follow, at last. 4 players, and we chose the new Colony quest, which in retrospect made it pretty similar to a one-shot game of Kingdom. It was a pretty nice game and ended in dire tragedy.

      \n Saturday:

      A playtest of my heist game. Two thieves in the swinging 1970s, one experimental passenger jet, a mid-air heist of some micro-fiche, and a hijacking foiled with some Die Hard/MacGuyver-esque action.

      I've found the right settings to make the thieves competent. Next up, to simplify the GM side of the game. I'm also doing a current reality tree analysis of why Weaknesses aren't working the way I want them to.

      But this was a good test: I've moved from a rough draft into an alpha draft.

      \n
      \nFall of Magic, another lovely game with a couple of people from my Story Games meetup and the girlfriend of one of them, playing storygames for the first time. And she was a rock star!

      In this telling, Caspian the swineherd lost two of his three pigs before reforging magic, Kabu the Last Crab-Singer and River the Scholar shared an autumnal romance (after River gave all the love she had once had for the Magus to him in order to revive him), and Piccolo the Giant faded from the world after transforming the last of Caspian's pigs into a new child for the new world. And magic was made into the simple magic of love and hope and things well made even as the Magus left the world.

      \n Refereed a first session of LotFP with essentially the same group that I play 5e with. First OSR experience for all of us.

      I think it took us about an hour to roll up a party of five; much quicker than in 5e. Even magic users are pretty quick, since their starting spells are random. I'm guessing that not everyone totally understands the encumbrance system yet, though.

      Started "A Stranger Storm" since I didn't have a ton of prep time and wanted to run something designed for the system, as opposed to me crafting something biased by experience with other systems. It's my first time running a module, and it's taking some getting used to. This is just an introduction, though; I fully intend for this campaign to open up into a sandbox after I get a read on the players and their characters.

      \nFellowship after a long layoff we finally return to play! We arrived at the Heir's city of Turan, afflicted by the Blight of Vince the Corrupter, and journeyed into the Blight to try and end it. Eslaf the Squire realized Calaf the Heir only has eyes for Arkona the Elf; Gorehanson the Orc killed some zombies; and we all took it on the chin in a very tough fight.

      Savage Worlds: Space 1889 The scenario was kinda subversively brilliant--it's the Space 1889 setting, but we're anarchists trying to defend the Interplanetary from the machinations of the British colonial authorities on Mars--and I quite liked my self-described Marxian intelligence expert, Mrs. Billings. But hey, it's still just Savage Worlds the the entire session was a combat scenario. It might be fun to visit the premise in Fate or similar.

      C'est la lutte finale

      L'Interplan\xe9taire
      sera le genre humain!

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nPolaris and Kingdom (technically that was last week and this week, but I'm cheating).

      Our Kingdom game was with new players, playing an actual Roman-ish empire. The Seer (Perspective, natch) has been conspiring with the foreign merchant (Touchstone) to secretly promote the worship of the Mother-Goddess, a cult from far-away lands. At the eleventh hour the merchant gets cold feet and shows he's just in it for the money, not the faith ("and so that's what the people feel\u2026"). The Seer, distraught that her hopes for the religion were pipe-dreams, throws herself from the high tower.

      Me: "Uh, you know you could have just changed to Touchstone and then the people would have been believers, just like you."

      Seer player: "\u2026 Dammit!"

      It was a funny moment at the table, but the player was actually happy with the tragic fail.

I shan't take the novels as gospel, but a) Victorian superheroes is already an alternate timeline and b) more readable than the wikipedia pages on the subject.

      \n Played one session of 5e, where our characters redeemed themselves from accidentally becoming outlaws quite a few sessions ago. Ran one session of LotFP, where a PC was killed by a lethal poison dart from another PC following some confusion caused by a changeling. The player wasn't too happy about it and thought the situation was unfair, which is kind of was, since changelings are by nature unfair.

      I'm not liking the experience of running a module at all, so even though the scenario isn't strictly finished I'm just going to abandon that ship as soon as we resolve the immediate problems. I'd like to angle for something like Eero's challenge-oriented D&D here, but honestly I'm not sure what will work best for this particular game.

      Edit: Except for the part where Eero's D&D is heavily reliant on modules for prep.

      Further edit: On the bright side, "do you throw the pillow with lethal intent?" came up more than once.

      \n\nLisa Padol \n\n\n

      \n\nJuly 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Kerberos Fate II: The Machinery of Life, using the Atomic Robo system and a scenario from 3rd ed Victoriana.

      Me: So, I've got this scenario, but whether we actually get to it or not is up to you.

      Player: I am Dorian Gray!

      Me: And you have an emu.

      Housemate of another player: Can I play?

      Me: You'll need a character.

      HoaP: I don't want anything complicated.

      Me: You can play the emu. I don't think we even need a character sheet for the emu.

      Player: Dorian named it Ozymandias!

      HoaP: It's a boy emu? Could it be Ozymandia?

      Me: I didn't check the gender of the emu. Dorian probably didn't either. You can also play one of Dr. Alexander's minions. (hands over sheet with four minions sketched out in sufficient but not overwhelming detail)

      Other Player: So, here I am, a clockwork faerie. Did I mention I have an invisible sister?

      HoaP: Invisible fairy?

      Me: Sure -- you want to be the invisible fairy? We'll figure out her character sheet as we go.

      Other Player: And you can also be tiny or normal sized. Oh, give her the stunt "I was there all along" -- you can pay a fate point to declare your character is in the scene.

      Me: (scribbles)

      Sometime later:

      Me: So, good news, bad news. The good news is Ladon's wife set out a thimble of tea for you.

      HoaP: Yay!

      Me: Bad news: Their 8 year old son is watching it like a hawk, ready to pounce, because he wants to play with you.

      HoaP: ...

      To my astonishment, we actually got about a third of the way through the scenario. (Why do I even have a scenario? Well, it helps folks to have something to bounce off of!)

      \n\nwdavidlewis \n\n\n

      \n
      \n
      \nThe Final Girl playing an ill-fated and ill-equipped rescue mission to a mining station taken over by a space borne plague that turned the miners into mutant spiders.

      We stretched the standard rules to six players which led to a little bit of awkwardness when roleplaying scenes - a few too many in the scene at one time, but we got the hang of it after playing a few scenes.

      Interestingly during one late scene playing the rookie crewman, I got dealt the Ad, As, Ac, a black K, Qc, Qs, and a 3. I pulled a number of people from the jaws (fangs?) of death that scene but in the end had to pick between saving the teenage stowaway or Carl the military man.

      We ended a little early with the rookie crewman, the young stowaway and the rescue robot ejecting from the station in a medical escape pod.

      We were all playing it for the first time and everyone agreed it was great fun.

      \n
      Been traveling and played Follow (two-player variant) while doing so. Feedback is coming, Ben.
      \nAs we were still on holiday last week, after Follow we played Steve Hickey's Left Coast. By the end of the 5th scene there were more than enough loose ends to warrant a second act, which we dutifully started and is still in progress. This may perhaps stretch out longer, making it our main "summer game" of the year, which we play a scene at a time while moving around.

      We're loving it! Although we have a feeling that we're partly on our own playing it as a 2-player game, as most of the instructions are written from the assumption of 3 or more players, and while some explicit changes from that are addressed, it doesn't look like all issues with a 2-player game are. We're going to try it with a larger group next Sunday so we can compare.

      \n
      \n
      \n Ran a third session of LotFP. At the beginning I presented the players with the option of switching over to World of Dungeons since I thought that might cater to their play style better, but they didn't take it since they didn't really care but also didn't want to reroll/convert their characters.

      There were two main developments from the session:\n
      1. Our cleric put Detect Evil and logic to good use in order to eliminate the remaining changelings.
      2. One of the our specialists was cursed (from the outset, by choice of the player) to make decisions essentially at random, and decided to put himself between some angry farmers and the last changeling. This eventually resulted in a pitchfork through the neck (a couple of great rolls from the farmers).
      \nI like not running the game from a module anymore, and I'm getting back in the groove of GMing while incorporating some new tricks and getting players to answer some questions about what happens. Cleric tries to convince farmers to be sympathetic toward the cursed specialist but rolls a 2 on Charisma. "So tell me how you screw this up, exactly?" \u2013 "I think I try to say that he's done some bad things but might still have a chance for redemption, but I accidentally overemphasize the bad things and they act before I can say my whole piece."

      I think I need to start moving up my fronts and throwing more complications at the PCs, since I don't think they have a good idea of what they'll want to accomplish next session. I also think we'll need a house rule for XP, since in LotFP it's overwhelmingly treasure-based (defeat 35 changelings by yourself and then talk to me about reaching level 2!), and no one seems to be a treasure hunter. We're also ignoring the period of unconsciousness when you drop to zero HP, since we don't really buy either the idea of being poked unconscious by a pitchfork or being head-smashed unconscious without sustaining a brain injury.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nMike_Sands \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Another episode of our The One Ring game. Investigating an orc lair, our heroes begin - in finest Tolkien tradition - tricking various goblins to their doom.

      After several episodes of this, the goblins catch on and gear up to get the intruders. Despite this, and with alert and battle-ready orcs waiting at the bottom of the stairs, our heroes still try to come up with ways to trick them. But no, at this point no lone goblin will be coming to see you in the stairwell for any reason. They may be stupid, but they're not that stupid.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nJames_Mullen \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2016 \n edited August 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Had the first session of my online game of The 'Hood last night: the four characters gelled really well, with an effective interweaving back story.

      Highlight of the session came from a completely unexpected missed roll right at the end: the thief decided to use a valuable car that had been entrusted to him by a creepy pyromaniac to deliver some stolen motherboards to his contact in the Russian Mafia. I tried to set up a discovery about a secret hidden within the car by suggesting that he was being followed, but his attempt to ditch the pursuit went spectacularly wrong and it got t-boned by another vehicle at the traffic lights! In one fell swoop, he got on the wrong side of two dangerous criminals who will both be coming after his blood for the destruction of their property. Nice.
      \n

      \n
      \n I played a two-hour session of Ryuutama and was underwhelmed, and I'm not sure if that's the game or the way it was run. It felt very procedural without much role-playing, as we made travelling rolls and camping rolls and such without any character development happening. Then there was a combat, which was the first place that felt like an RPG. I didn't understand the role of the dragon spirit that was with us, who never really interacted.

      \n
      \nFellowship, my last session for a couple weeks as I'm on holiday next week. We journeyed to the mountains of the Orc Range in search of the mushroom that is Vince the Corrupter's weakness, fought some earth golems, and barely escaped in one piece. And this:

      Arkona the Elf, to the Heir: The love of a mortal for an elf is a dangerous thing...

      The Heir, flustered: Oh...I...

      Arkona: For the elf. We were born before the sun. When it rose, it flamed our hearts with desire. But the sun fades all things. Each generation of elves is weaker than the last. But humans...every generation of humans have the same intensity at heart. Perhaps not as strong as it was for the elves, in the beginning, but it stays the same generation after generation. When a mortal loves an elf...he awakens that fire in her heart. And that is a dangerous thing. For we did many great things in those days...but they were terrible things.

      \n I was at a local convention, where I hosted games of:
      - Firebrands (it really comes into its own with 4 players: the hiccups I've experienced with just 3 weren't felt this time at all),
      - Left Coast (I wished to try this one with a larger number of players as well, and succeeded; some of my expectations were met, but some other things didn't change from a 2-player game as much as I had expected),
      - and a playtest of my own The Truth Dies with Them (I'd call it a very successful alpha run).

      I played almost full-time over the weekend and had a great time.

      \n A bunch of stuff at GenCon, including Dread: Gremlins, which was astonishingly fun, as we all aimed for the same brand of comic horror.

      Today, Kerberos Fate, Atomic Robo Remix. Where the solidly middle class herbalist is very glad they're getting paid well, Dorian Gray is much more excited at getting complimentary circus tickets. We'll likely wrap this scenario next time, as we came fairly close tonight. And I need to remember that Dr. Alexander has two minions with him, which means I get two extra fate points for the relevant scenes. (This was the compromise we came to, with me arguing that the player should not waste a Mode defining skills he only got to have with the appropriate minion, and the player wanting to pay for things properly, but not finding a way to do so affordably within the rules.)

      \n First game of Fall of Magic with one other player. Fun game but with only two of us and the open structure of the narrative, we both had a lot to carry. I was Caspian the Golem of Ravenhall and I established him as long silent watcher over the scrying pool that was awakened by the Magus. Which was kind of a passive character. Made it somewhat difficult to establish really good scenes. I kind of had to back my way into them. A third player would have made that easier I think. I was in the process of trying to engineer an arc that would turn him into more of a leader when we ran out of time.

      Definitely a fun one. Hope to try it again with three or four players next time.

      \n Short and somewhat awkward session of LotFP that mainly involved the characters meeting up with replacement PCs (after PC death from previous sessions) and getting on the road toward something tangible. I threw a couple of potential fronts at them and they bit on the second hook. I like the system for what it's designed for. but I feel like it's not really conducive to the sort of play the players are going for, or maybe I should be doing different things as a GM. I feel like we'd be better served by playing something more character-driven and less party-reliant.

      \n Played Microscope - second time for me and first time for the other two players.

      We built a history of a civilization where superpowers were discovered when the world was in a Bronze Age level of technology and went until it the people flipped into a new species. It played out as a kind of cross between X-Men and elements of Julian May's Metapsychic Rebellion series. Coercion was a power and the whole history was a bit dark with prejudice and coercion playing key roles throughout the history. There were a lot of dark circles on the table when we got done.

      where superpowers were discovered when the world was in a Bronze Age level of technology and went until it the people flipped into a new species
      \nSweeeeeeeet!

      Continuing my Polaris streak at Story Games Seattle. This last game started with a mix of inexperienced and exhausted players and should have been a slog (Polaris is a lot to learn). Instead of giving up we hunkered down and embraced not planning, just playing to see what happens next, and the game took off like a frickin' rocket.

      I am so pleased with the human race.

      \n
      Continuing my Polaris streak at Story Games Seattle. This last game started with a mix of inexperienced and exhausted players and should have been a slog (Polaris is a lot to learn). Instead of giving up we hunkered down and embraced not planning, just playing to see what happens next, and the game took off like a frickin' rocket.
      \nThe thing about Polaris is that it's very well written. When my group played it, we kept being surprised that every time we weren't sure what we were supposed to do, we found the answer in the rulebook, quickly and clearly. This should be the norm, of course, but it isn't.

      \nFellowship: wherein a new Harbinger arrived on the seen, and with the Orc and Elf battled a dragon in the Mines of Marceline only to have Vince the Overlord show up and kick everyone's ass. He needs to go down. Hard.

      Dungeon World: The Paladin and the Fighter found the Dragon Cult in the town of Drakensburg: turns out that they originally evolved to protect half of a dragon-killing artifact, although a splinter group thinks their power comes from the dragon and are worshiping it.

      Meanwhile, the Thief and the Ranger tried to broker a peace between the bandits and the Orc Third Legion; both sides used the negotiation as a chance to ambush the other side, until the dragon showed up. It played a deadly game of cat and mouse with them; the Ranger talked to the long-dead spirit of a king whose urn they found in a funeral boat inside the mountain (they used it to escape.) Finally reunited, everyone is now setting off to fight the dragon...

      Fun!

      \n\nJason_Morningstar \n\n\n

      \n\nAugust 2016 \n edited August 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I ran some Dogs in the Vineyard last night for the first time in ages! It was pretty cool. I chose Coachwhip Cutoff and just followed my friends' lead. Since it was our first town I didn't push the badness too hard, and when it became obvious that the Dogs just weren't seeing any evidence of demonic influence I scaled everything way back. There's plenty to chew on without a sorceress with snakes for hair.

      It was satisfying to see them firmly side with what would have (and might yet be) the cult, though! They divorced sister Carmel and sent her as chaperone with the love birds Dinah (who is still washing her husband's death-blood off her hands) and David (who is, at least technically, Dinah's son) off to Bridal Falls City to reunite Dinah with her parents, with the tacit understanding that David would ask for her (bloody) hand in marriage there. Everybody else in town got exactly the opposite of what they wanted.\ufeff

      That was my town; next week someone else runs a new town.
      \n

      \n
      \n We got some answers in the Diaspora game, which is satisfying, even if they boil down to, "Yeah, the cluster is basically fucked." We have an energy being about to give birth and keep a promise to another energy being (whom I think we killed -- at the being's own request) which will apparently involve destroying a world that's a living tree, a plague that's mutating faster than it can be cured, nasty people trying to take over the cluster, and the people we're trying to help evacuate their planet on the sly, under the nasty people's noses.

      It seems the folks we're trying to help have... er, an... annual holiday, yes (which they didn't celebrate last year, or the year before, or...) when they leave the planet and take the space elevator up to the space station, yes.

      We've a session in a couple of weeks, so maybe we'll see how things boil down.

      \n
      \n
      \n My wife ran some Moldvay D&D for us on Monday night. It's a satisfying exploration game. Brutal time pressure and strong procedurals.

      Two new players (previously played 4th and 5th ed) had some trouble. Near the end of the session the light bulbs clicked on.

      Our slightly drifted rules worked like a charm. We hack very carefully to keep with the spirit and intent of the rules.

      \n
      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Jason,

      Your Dogs game sounds quite interesting. One of my favourite Dogs games was a series of sessions I ran for a group of players who had never played Dogs. They were keen to develop some backstory for their characters, so I structured the game as a series of visits. Each week this brand-new group of Dogs would visit one of their hometowns. This made a great dynamic for the game, where the players contributed a fair bit to the design of each Town (I would ask them to tell me a little about a significant person in their family), and then often came to loggerheads about the issues in each particular Town. (For instance, three of the Dogs would try to convince the fourth that his father needed to be removed from his role as Steward, or they would argue over the necessity to punish another Dog's old childhood crush.)

      I used a semi-random process to inject a few twists into each Town (as changes which took place while they were off training to be Dogs), and it was very satisfying.

      ---

      This week, I ran a storytelling game I wrote about ten years ago (called "Musette") for a small group of non-gamers who got really excited about Fiasco.

      Musette is a very structured, procedural game in terms of the players' roles, which leaves a lot of room to explore the fiction itself. (Kind of like Microscope in terms of design balance, although it drives the drama a lot more, and has the potential to be played competitively.)

      Our story was a dystopian near future with a strict separation between rich and poor, and the new introduction of sentient robots as an additional "lower class". One of the protagonists was a naive and misunderstood robot who was blamed for a recent riot. The others were a poor old woman who was sitting on incredible wealth she wasn't allowed to legally access (and her conflict with her upper-class daughter, who wanted the money), and an angry aging banker who was so angry at the whole system that he wanted to bring down the very bank he'd spent his whole life working for.

      We had a lovely social shaming dynamic going on between humans (who had proper, full names) and robots (who had only a code name and number, no last name, but tried desperately to earn a "human" name).

      In the end, the old banker used the robot's ("KVN436", who wished to be called "Kevin") naivete to trick him into setting up a massive electronic bank robbery. This sparked a second riot, wherein "KVN" was, quite tragically (as he was innocent), killed by a mob of angry citizens throwing bricks.

      However, before dying, "KVN" managed to explain to the daughter that the true situation behind the first riots was a setup. He was innocent, and his dying words revealed to her a massive conspiracy working behind the scenes.

      The old lady and her daughter were reunited in the hospital and came to terms with their lost fortune. Unfortunately, the daughter took a blow to the head during the riot and couldn't remember what "KVN" had told her in his last moments.

      The old banker was the only one to truly come out ahead: he retired with the stolen money and lived out the rest of his days on a tropical island.

      I was really pleasantly surprised by how immediately the players grasped the idea of story gaming and plunged into it head on. We had a great time, finished exactly on time (2 1/2 hours - I've played my own game enough to be able to pace it accurately these days), and they are definitely hooked on the idea of story gaming. We'll definitely be doing it again.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nwdavidlewis \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Our group played Geiger Counter for the first time (for all of us). Our story took place in an old unused prison on an island. For reasons related to our goals, we missed the ferry home and had to survive the night.

      Generally went well but with late arrivals we got started late. This plus the fact we're all new led to some not unexpected pacing problems. We did get all the way through and defeated the menace by surviving the night. We learned a bunch about the game and the next one should go smoother (the only way to get that first game behind you is to play it).
      \n

      My wife ran some Moldvay D&D for us on Monday night. It's a satisfying exploration game. Brutal time pressure and strong procedurals.
      ...
      Our slightly drifted rules worked like a charm. We hack very carefully to keep with the spirit and intent of the rules.
      \nPlease make a thread! I just can't get the procedural routine down strongly enough as a DM to keep play ticking over. Is it the hack that makes it work, or just polished DM skills?

      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nAugust 2016 \n edited August 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      My wife ran some Moldvay D&D for us on Monday night. It's a satisfying exploration game. Brutal time pressure and strong procedurals.

      Two new players (previously played 4th and 5th ed) had some trouble. Near the end of the session the light bulbs clicked on.
      \nI still occasionally run Moldvay, usually with Keep on the Borderlands. I've also watched experienced players have a really hard time getting it, and keeping their characters alive. It's fun when you see the light bulbs click on!

      * * *


      This last week or so, I ran three sessions of my D&D 5e "social sandbox" setting, City of Brass. Two of the sessions were "full-length" (four-hour) sessions, which was new for the campaign, and the other one was a typical two-hour game.

      Two cool things to talk about.

      Total Party Kill. In the last game on Saturday, four PCs made some bad decisions and got themselves killed towards the end of the fourth hour. This was basically an escalation of gang violence that could have been avoided by all parties at any number of points.

      PC vs. PC Conflict. In a previous game, Grog (half-orc, half-giant-ant barbarian) had decided to subsidize NPC Hengritte's spirit potion addiction, as a sort of stopgap solution to keep her from selling them to kids to fund her habit. He would buy all the potions instead of the kids, basically.

      In a later session, another group of PCs decided this was bullshit and went to confront him, but the player wasn't present. I played Grog as an NPC, and he got his ass whooped and they basically kidnapped Hengritte and put her on lockdown while they tried to break her addiction.

      To be fair to Grog's player, I have split the timelines. When Grog's player shows up, we will replay that whole scene, with me playing the other PCs as NPCs (because they most certainly won't be there--the two play groups are totally bifurcated). If Grog gets his ass whooped again in a similar fashion, then the timelines merge and all is fine. Otherwise, I just have two divergent timelines. The player groups will almost assuredly never play together.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n
      Our group played Geiger Counter for the first time (for all of us).
      \nWhich version?
      \nThe August 2008 beta edition with the octopus on the front page. I'm trying to get other people in the group facilitating and this was the version the facilitator had studied - I wasn't going to try and turn us in another direction. Plus, I'm interested in playing several games of both to see how they work. I suspect as we get more familiar, we'll try both.

      I recall reading somewhere that you prefer the alpha version, though I could be mistaken. I'd be interested in your experiences with the differences, though that might be better for a separate thread, a comment on my profile (however that works on this board) or even e-mail.

      \nDogs in the Vineyard and Amos and Boaz are confronting a straight-up sorcerer for the first time in their young lives. The man is flush with power. Amos and Boaz tell him what he has to do and he says "I think you better leave now", and they do, and the flames guttering in his lamps seem to mock them.

      But before he leaves Amos says "I have seen the wicked in great power, and spreading himself like a green bay tree. We'll see you again, sir, and soon."

      And they go and get their guns and try to remember what they'd been taught about casting out demons.\ufeff

      \nFellowship: The last two sessions saw the Fellowship strike back against the Overlord, seizing two Places of Power--Vince has been revealed as a twisted, withered husk (not the powerful figure he presented when his Frightening stat was working), and shattering the Crystal Skull of the Grimwood broke the spell that made him Untouchable.

      Now the Fellowship returns to the Elven woods, for the final confrontation with the Overlord. Arkona the Elf is now a weakness to the Overlord; and she prepares to lead her people into oblivion to overthrow Vince and heal the break in the world the Elves created.

      Coming up: The conclusion to the Dungeon World three shot, and then very soon a new Trail of Cthulhu campaign (with Jimmy Wright!) and probably a run of Masks of Nyarlathotep transposed to the end of the 1960s. Groovy, man.

      \n\nwdavidlewis \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nSeptember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Our group played our second game of Microscope with two of us having played a few weeks ago and two new players. We took one of the seeds in the rulebook about secret societies controlling the course of civilization.

      Initially we had a bunch of events that were all large scale and political in nature but when we started adding named characters, stories got a lot more intimate. The concept of souls and soul transference got introduced (bringing us to the brink of playing with immortality) and we ended with a Romeo & Juliet couple from different secret societies threading their way through our history. It actually worked very well and allowed us to close with a very human story.

      It was interesting and pleasantly surprising that the whole souls aspect (which turned out to be a major part of the history) was completely unknown at the beginning.

      Great fun. Microscope is turning into a group favorite.
      \n

      \n\nJason_Lutes \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nSeptember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I've been running a *World hack of The Dracula Dossier, finished our... 8th? session last week. It's interesting and challenging for me because I prefer a totally analog game table but for this the iPads and iPhones are in full play. It's super useful for a modern-day campaign, and fun to make use of Google maps and other online resources on the fly, but I really hate having any of my attention on a device instead of the players or my hard prep.

      The game itself is fun and everyone is enjoying themselves as they find themselves on the run across Eastern Europe, contacts and bank accounts folding in their wake as they try to make sense of the overlap between vampire R&D and the War on Terror. A lot of planning time spent before infiltrating black sites, and a ton of hacking. The combo of hacking with physical infiltration is a great thing.

      The main drawback I'm finding to the DD's "improvisational campaign structure" is that I have to do like 10x the homework than I would have to do in a more structured setting.
      \n
      \n

      Explosion in Space, the Russian LARP brought to English by Jason Morningstar and James Stuart; with James facilitating! Very good stuff. Everyone died and my character articulated the human condition with one of her last utterances: "Oh god, I f***ed up."

      \n Kerberos Club this week, using Atomic Robo's system and Victoriania's scenario. Ladon went to his father's wife to discuss his daughter, and we realized that Ladon's skills as a sharp bargainer are due to dealing with his sidhe younger brother, which makes so much sense. Dr. Alexander examined a corpse that was moving after the body's death, and Felicity turned into a clockwork dog to follow its scent and is now taking the shape of the dead woman because what could possibly go wrong playing bait, right? Meanwhile, Dorian Gray agrees with Lady Miriam that the sculptor accused of murder simply must be saved from execution! Who cares whether he did the deed or not? They can just execute some nobody who isn't a brilliant artist!

      \n I played Lacuna. It was a GM + me + another guy. I liked the setting and the surreal dream stuff going on. I disliked the total lack of mechanical support for what seemed to be an investigation plot. As I told our GM, it seemed that either no roads led to the MacGuffin, or all roads led to the MacGuffin. I didn't feel like I was making meaningful choices about the investigation plot, but realized later that the game was probably not about the mission as much as it's about everything else.

      \n\nwdavidlewis \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nSeptember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n We played a game of House of Reeds. Our "house" was a once celebrated brothel on Rt 66 in the early 70's. The sexual revolution was ending and Rt 66 was dying. Our house had seen better days and it's star was fading. We ended up working through the interpersonal dynamics of the staff.

      It was a rough time for our little family as the first round, we drew a departure card and then a disaster card (which reshuffles). The next round, departure followed by disaster. Happened a round or two after that as well. Tough times but we had some very poignant moments.

      Despite the potential, it was a very tame game. Stayed PG-13 with a few ventures into R territory. We all liked the setup so much, we may revisit it for a later game.

      We flirted with some tone issues, but all in all, one of my most satisfying story games so far.
      \n

      \n I played The Final Voyage of The Selene via Hangouts yesterday as the Juve Mahler character, with an agenda to win the attention of Lieutenant Kazarian as a kind of surrogate parent. I died when Professor Carris' insane teleportation experiment caused a critical feedback loop and blew up The Selene, so I got a legacy flash-forward to show admiralty troops liberating other juveniles from the state-run 'school' that Mahler had escaped from.

      I've been thoroughly enjoying Hangout gaming through the summer and getting a feel for best practices, so I think more Gauntlet Hangouts games are on the cards for the future.

      \n\nFelan \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nSeptember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nDungeon World one-shot using Funnel World. Six randomly-generated level zero villagers, a crofter, a woodsman, a stoic tax collector, a hunter, an innkeeper and, most hilariously, a large-handed, very strong, very stupid scribe, strike out to reclaim the stolen Hand of St. Ignys in the midst of an apocalyptic storm. They follow a clue to the forbidden Standing Stones and learn that ghostly creatures from beyond the Black Gates are breaking through. One villager dies by ghostly bat swarm. They eventually catch up with the Paladin and Acolyte of St. Ignys who stole the Hand, and learn that the local cleric has been shirking his responsibilities and not bothering to perform the proper rituals to keep evil at bay, and that the Hand must be returned to the Cathedral along with the other relics to be used in a last-ditch effort to prevent the coming darkness. The villagers, foolish peasants that they are, decide that no high-talking city folk are going to steal their local relic, and try to take it back by force, resulting in the death of another villager. Ultimately the acolyte rushes off with the Hand and the Paladin begrudgingly sacrifices herself to save the (completely undeserving) remaining villagers from the ghosts, and they rush back home to tell their tale.

      Play of the game: the crofter tries to rescue the hunter from a tar pit with a pitchfork, rolls snake eyes, and accidentally stabs her instead.

      \nFiasco Touring Rock Band: Lou the head roadie had issues. Her "friend" Tommie back in St Paul is on her case, she's scared the band thinks knows she's gay, the old keyboardist is still hanging around, and the new keyboardist is some Swedish tech head who wants to set himself on fire for the Youtube hits. Everything spins out of control at a gig in a coffeeshop in Idaho, attended by the local Buddhist monks, that somehow manages not to turn into the latest pyrotechnic disaster.

      A lot of fun (I was Lou), and I introduced three people to Fiasco.

      Fellowship the Finale! Arkona the Elf seals the fate of her people as the Fellowship finally confronts Vince face to face. Gorehanson slays the Overlord with his necrotic sword, the squire-turned-knight receives what small virtue remains in the province of the elf queen, the heir brings the knight to his realm, and the elves lay down the stewardship of the world and fade out of existence, healing the wound they inflicted on it.

      Fellowship was a lot of fun; it would probably work better with longer sessions in a less noisy environment, but it mostly sang. (And I know there's a Star Wars hack in there, I just haven't teased it out yet.)

      Next up: the final leg in my Trail of Cthulhu trilogy opens with the wedding of Jimmy Wright's niece to the son of Governor Bradley Grey of California.

      \n\nOctober 2016 \n

      \n
      \n
      \n I ran yet another two-hour bar session of City of Brass for D&D 5e.

      I only mention it here because I totally fucked up yet the players seemed to have a great time.

      I totally fucked up because I have a procedure for running this social justice sandbox, and I skipped half of it. I had added "you find a trap door to a basement" to my random events table, and that led to a sort of dungeon adventure that was not de rigeur for City of Brass, and despite it being only two rooms, it ate 90 minutes of the two hour slot, and I was rushed and the 8 PM band started playing 30 minutes early.

      I just totally forgot to have the characters tell me where they were eating and make community morale rolls. It's important to the rhythm and mood of the game.

      On the bright side, they had fun killing a giant wasp, and I had fun describing how Parker's paladin got paralyzed by a bad sting, with very graphic descriptions that I'll forego now because there are probably bee-phobic people reading. Love you.

      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Troels Ken Pedersen's Sorcery in the Forest is in this collection.

      The Lofty Beacons.

      The rest are in playtest - Starship Freeform is a larp where you play a space opera crew. The idea of atomizing authority out-of-game using the logical stuff in-game characters are responsible for is genius. I played the Captain and drove plot. The weapons officer was responsible for making everything dangerous. The ship's counselor overlaid emotions on every scene. Jay Treat is still working on it but it is great.

      The Liberation of Sisyphus is a sci-fi reskin of Montsegur 1244, also in playtest, really sharp. Instead of burning you go down swinging. Instead of recanting you go back to prison on Mars.

      \n\nJeff_B_Slater \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nOctober 2016 \n edited October 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n We had our second meeting for Indie RPGs & Story Games SLC...the first week I was sick and couldn't make it, but apparently they had a very lively and successful game of Fiasco...
      This week we played a game developed by one of our members (Brandon Nelson) called Mongrel 2.0. Brandon has been playing traditional RPGs for a long time and is trying to break out of the mold. His game is fairly traditional and not too narrative, but he had a resolution mechanic that gave you the option to choose how much of your dice pool was allocated to a given task. If you allocated all of your dice you would have a greater chance of success at that task but other things might be neglected; for example, you could roll all of your dice to aim your laser gun but you might not be very aware of other dangers in your environment. This mechanic didn't seem very flushed out or strictly defined; it seemed rather slippery and up to Brandon's impromptu interpretation. That said, I never really got a chance to "look under the hood" so maybe things are more defined then they seem. The dice mechanics were definitely different...players would role a combo of d30s, d20s, d10s, and d6s hoping to roll higher than a target number (say a 7 for example) on every die, and the GM would also roll for this number but using a d6 dice pool that was variable in regards to the number of die rolled depending on the particular roll--needless to say, it was a confounding and strange system and unfortunately I didn't have time to ask Brandon questions about it afterwords.
      We played a one-shot sci-fi game with a lot of intrigue. Brandon created pre-generated characters with motives, secrets, backgrounds, etc. (but these were not part of the actual character sheets or mechanics). Brandon only had about a day to prepare and I was super impressed with his ability to weave a tangled web of intrigue. The session started slow being that we were all playing two characters and had a lot of info to absorb. It got interesting and very PVP soon as we all began mingling at a party of who's who in a pressure controled mansion on a moon. I made a bunch of wrong assumptions that made me plant drugs in a bathroom, pull a later gun on another PC and accuse him of colluding with some space anarchists that attacked the moon home we were in and other crazy stuff. It was very interesting and fun even though it took a little bit to warm up, but eventually things started to snow ball. One of my characters had to get info from one of my other characters so I had her get him high (he was a drug addict) and flirt and pretend to be interested in him; it was kind of weird flirting with a loser (the guy was essentially Brad Pitt's character in Burn After Reading) while playing a woman...I think it gave me a little bit of empathy for the fairer sex Two of the other players were amazing at roleplaying and really provided a lot of entertainment. The ending was a little underwhelming because we ran an hour over; we ended up in a "safe-room" in the mansion and radioed for help and the space anarchists and another group who was trying to overthrow the moons security in a coup basically bailed to avoid being caught by those we sent out the signal to...sorry if this is confusing...I'm leaving a lot out Anyway, the intrigue seemed to work because of the character's Brandon had made and the setup, despite the game having no machanics that worked to specifically create intrigue. The game design felt very traditional, where most of what the game does is a result of the GMs work rather than an outcome of the games design. It was fun, I think that Brandon is a good GM and it will be very satisfying to see his world expand as he is introduced to new design approaches
      \n

      \n\nOctober 2016 \n
      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n A dungeon themed game of Forget Me Not and The Quiet Year.

      The Quiet Year had a lot of politics in it (funny timing, that). We were living in an underground complex and many of the questions revolved around what kind of government were we going to have. Democracy vs autocratic? How do we deal with dissent? How do we punish our criminals? And then the obvious question of how many of our limited resources to we expend going outside? Good game but it was a downhill slide all the way.

      One complaint was that without roleplaying, there isn't as much connection to the community. Something we're working on figuring out as a group.

      \n Last night, I ran Basic D&D (Moldvay, 1980) and Keep on the Borderland for four players. Three had never played that edition. One had played it fairly extensively, despite being in his mid-thirties, and knew the module well, too. I changed up enough stuff to keep him guessing.

      They did very well, convincing a dozen of the local peasant types to join them in a quest for revenge against the goblins that had razed their farmsteads. But random encounters are brutal, and their forays into the wilderness cut their numbers down. Eventually, they made it to the Caves of Chaos, falling into area I (the minotaur's labyrinth) and barely escaping with their lives, and then into area G, where they naively accepted the food offerings from the three bugbears, who turned on the party and first slew the 2-hit-point magic-user, then slew the fighter. The rest fled.

      Over the weekend I was at TravellerCon. I ran Mongoose Traveller (1e). I had no new material ready, so I ran my "Merchants of Dennis" and "Independence 2776" adventures, mentioned upthread. The con attracts old Traveller grognards, but they seemed to enjoy my neo-Traveller offerings.

      \n\nLisa Padol \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nOctober 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Played Hunter S. Thompson in a playtest of "All Along the Watchtower", which was a lot of fun.

      Ran Kerberos: Machinery of Life, using our hack of the Atomic Robo rules.

      I thought the game was about Our Heroes rescuing a nurse from dastardly villains doing horrible medical experiments. And yes, that all happened (with a lot of rolling for crap on my end), including Felicity turning into a clockwork white stallion and charging out of the villain's lair with the nurse strapped to a table on her back, and then charging back in with Dorian Gray on her back.

      But apparently, what this session was about was Dorian getting jumped, much to his delight, by denizens of one of London's seedier neighborhoods.

      GM: So, you're basically letting them beat you and rob you?

      Dorian: Yes! Wait, my food diary!

      At Dr. Alexander's request (he's fascinated by Dorian's physiology), Dorian started keeping a food diary. So, when the robbers tried to take it, he grabbed it and protested, thus convincing them that it must be valuable.

      Dorian's shouts were heard by the doctor and the fourth member of the group, Ladon.

      Ladon (sighing): I told you Dorian would happen to somebody.

      The doctor was accompanied by Caesar, his created Child and bodyguard. They tried to intimidate the robbers into leaving.

      And now, I started rolling well. So, Caesar starting fighting all but one of them, while the exception was in a tug of war with Dorian over the food diary.

      Ladon sighed and tried to snatch the diary with a vine whip.

      And again, I rolled well. Not astonishingly well, and these guys -- I was eyeballing it, not actually statting them out, but I was figuring, okay, they're thugs, so figure a +2 in the relevant fighty skill, and I rolled two +s on the dice.

      So, there was now a three-way tug of war between Thug, Dorian, and Ladon.

      Felicity's player was actually running late, but we figured comedic timing meant she should show up about now. When the player arrived, we described what she saw. The clockwork woman hit the thug on the head with her fist, and the player rolled a 6 to my total of 2.

      Kerberos. There's the A plot (nefarious schemes), the B plot (social issues ranging from marriage to justice for all), and the thing we actually focus on (whatever zany thing catches our attention, often something I just kind of tossed out there, though this one was actually in the scenario).
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n
      \nTrail of Cthulhu: I ran a playtest of All Along the Watchtower with a certain storygamer playing Dr. Hunter S. Thompson. I think my favorite bits were: Pigasus the pig/mythos entity taking Jimi Hendrix to see Azathoth and then discussing whether or not he could put a Tristan chord in the song he would write about it; and E Howard Hunt telling Hunter that the Field Museum was "a nexus of occult energies" and to "not doubt that I can make your head explode at 500 paces."

      Coming up, the second session of my "Tatters of the King, except in Trail of Cthulhu and set in 1950s America, and with different main characters" campaign.

      \n\nJeff_B_Slater \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nOctober 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Here is a recap of last week's game of Follow. We played Follow by Ben Robbins. I had a lot of fun. We roleplayed and created a super crazy and creative story you can read bellow:

      Our mission was to colonize a new planet. Our group was a fledgling penal colony: felons, outcasts, and the politically inconvenient. The planet is Gulag; an ancient cluster of space junk coalesced together by gravity through the eons. In fact, the planet may only be made of space junk.
      There were immediate rumors about getting rid of the weak, of leaving them on the planet\u2019s surface so the strong could then take the ship. The planet surface turned out to be highly electrically charged--portions of the planet highly alkaline, others strongly acidic, like a big ol' battery.
      It wasn't clear if the planet could be made to grow plant life or not. By the end of the first challenge the colony's farmer had indeed stolen the ship, and the colony had been started in a bad location.
      Alkaline spiders were picking off colonists. Perhaps these spiders were sentient, it wasn't clear. The spiders had been seen praying by one of the crew members but it was also speculated that it may have also been some form of a mating ritual. Acid launchers were created, but they were ineffective. The crew captured some of these spiders and locked them up in a containment chamber.
      A recreational "pharmaceuticals" expert stumbled on a secret, a euphoric drug that allowed him to live among the spiders. He and a felon sent to tail him left the colony and joined the spiders. Apparently, when humans who took the drug were near the spiders the spiders became docile; eventually these two crew members became the kings of the spiders and were even worshiped by them.
      As we approached the end, the colonists were still dying, and the food and oxygen running out fast. Experiments were made (on willing and unwilling subjects) with eating the planets' flora and fauna. The captured spider\u2019s biology was too different from humans to test the flora and fauna on. Eating the flora caused the human subjects a euphoric painless death. Eating the fauna caused a painful non-euphoric death.
      Mania and panic set in. All the colonists died, except those who had abandoned the mission.
      \n
      \n

      \n\nPaul_T \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nOctober 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Interesting!

      Is it also a fun game for people who don't want things to get all weird, or is that part of its charm?
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nJeff_B_Slater \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nOctober 2016 \n edited October 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      Interesting!

      Is it also a fun game for people who don't want things to get all weird, or is that part of its charm?
      \nI think it's part of it's charm. But I think since the narrative authority is shared and people are coming up with ideas on the fly, and there's no way to veto, there is going to be a little craziness. People who don't play story games might not like this, but I think it makes things more creative. Of course every game isn't gonna be crazy and if you played with people who really wanted the story to be down to earth, and you stated that before hand, then you could make the story that way For example, you could say: we are really going for realism, this is what that means etc--and then start playing.
      \n

      \n Played Roguish as a Wizard. Probably need to emphasize the narrative aspects more than we did (though I did fight a fairly epic battle with a squid shaman). We actually ended up with a reasonably well structured story out of the random elements.

      Also played my first game of Shooting the Moon. There were two of us and we used the original rules rather than the new ones as I want to see what (if any) changes were made. A bumpy start but it got smoother as we went along. We were a novice witch and a senior warlock competing for the affections of our coven leader during a war with another coven. We ended up in a tie so we lost the war and neither one of us won his heart.

      \n\nFelan \n\n\n
