      \n\nOctober 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n First part of an epic, campaign-ending battle in my long-running game that began as a Pathfinder game using the Council of Thieves Adventure Path and was converted to Dungeon World after that). With help from folks here on Story-Games I came up with a nice, lightweight battle mini/metagame to show their influence as powerful heroes on a big, chaotic battlefield.

      For a laugh I put together an inspirational speech for one of the commanding officers on their side to recite, cobbled together from fragments of a half-dozen famous movie speeches:\n
      Today, we have no choice but to march against Thrune herself, and end this war the only way it could have ended: by freeing every slave in Cheliax! A day may come, when the courage of free people fails. But it is not this day! This day we fight! This day we rescue a world from diabolism and tyranny and usher in a future brighter than anything we can imagine. They may take our lives, but they'll never take our freedom! From this day to the ending of the world, we shall be remembered - we few, we happy few! Soon now, we will meet them face-to-face. While we stand together, no invader shall pass. Let them come with the armies of Hell; they will not pass! And when this day of battle is ended, we meet again in heaven or on the field of victory. FOR FREE CHELIAX!
      \n

      \n We played a three-player version of my game, Thus began the Adventures of Eowyn..., although without any Lord of the Rings colour, just generic dark ages fantasy.

      It was quite successful so far, although I'm having trouble getting the players to commit to courses of action for their characters, which is making it challenging to GM for them. I'd prefer to be more reactive as GM, instead of throwing material at them all the time. We'll see if the second session will be better.

      \n\nOctober 2016 \n

      \n
      \nTrail of Cthulhu: two games here.

      First The Unanswered Question, my "Tatters of the King in the '50s" reboot/mix. The Investigators made a return visit to Savannah's Joy Grove...er, Mental Rest Facility (last seen in Eternal Lies ) then headed to San Francisco arriving in time to hear Ginsberg read from Howl for the first time. Temperance got an armored RV from her father, Candace hung with the Beats, Charleston met a character from "The Neurastheniac" and a memorable supporting character met a sad end in Jimmy's arms.

      Then part 2 of All Along the Watchtower. Gordon Liddy entered the lists; he and Hendrix were made champions by Nixon and Leary, respectively. Gary Gygax got a polearm, shoggoths amused themselves immensely, and Kissinger realized that all they had accomplished was to make clear the way for the Actor. But that's another story.

      \n Gosh! I've just catched up with this thread after, what, maybe 3 months' absence from it? It was hard work, but totally worth it.
      I'm thinking, maybe starting Nov 1st we should re-start a new "What did you play this week" thread every month, like we used to do with the Stuff to Watch threads. Makes it easier for people to join in (or join back after skipping some).

      For me, last Sunday was the 7th session (in a row! that's quite impressive for our standards) in a game of Freebooting Venus which is nowhere near ending soon, I believe. I'm the GM and the players are exactly the same people I've had my last, long-running, very satisfying game of Apocalypse World with. Not that the tone of the game is anywhere near AW, despite some shared tech - it's a very different beast. I've been posting extensively about our game on the FV forums, especially this thread: A game I love to prep for -- but also Items of historical significance and magical potency, The helpless are hard to kill and several more.

      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Getting this out of the way since my Metatopia after action report will be longer...

      Bluebeard's Bride, a last minute replacement game for our Meetup (the guy who was going to run Dread got sick.) I only had two players, so we missed some of the interplay between characters that makes the game special. Still a good time, with me pitching out some decent horrors. Both players identify as male, so there were some interesting reactions, IMHO--quite a bit of willingness to directly confront the horrors. Next time I run this I will bring more pain when that happens.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nwdavidlewis \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nMurderous Ghosts - twice. First as MC, then we flipped it around and then as the player.

      The first game went pretty fast. In the second room, the player opted to draw with a 15 and busted. Thus he was suddenly murdered by a ghost (imagine). We didn't really understand the rhythm of the game. The second time went much better. We understood the mechanics and the rhythm.

      I really like the pulls in different directions. Midway through the game, I realized I could "tap out" but that would just be surviving, not winning. I wanted to push it and try to win. I did push it and with a few good draws, I managed to survive and escape the factory.

      This is a really fun, yet creepy game. We both enjoyed it.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      I'm playing in a very slow moving PbP game with OKULT mechanics, but some sort of African inspired fantasy setting.
      \nThat's interesting! Wilhelm, is this your first attempt adapting Okult to different themes/settings, or have you done it before? Do you retain the basic structure of having two intertwined timelines, the present time scenes and the flashback ones, or have you replaced it with something else for this fantasy game? Do you use some sort of predefined arc, like the escalation from Normal to Terrifying in the horror game?

      \n\nWilhelm \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      I'm playing in a very slow moving PbP game with OKULT mechanics, but some sort of African inspired fantasy setting.
      \nThat's interesting! Wilhelm, is this your first attempt adapting Okult to different themes/settings, or have you done it before? ...
      \nWe have played an Alien/Aliens inspired game before, it used the full mechanics, except we didn't flashback to the teenage years, but to events on Earth before cryo sleep.

      This game is a more loose adaption. There are flashbacks, but they are not focused on a specific period, just "before the game started". And there is no predefined arc yet, I might introduce it once the players have a better grasp of how the game works.

      I still am enjoying, as GM, that the campaign is lightly enough plotted that we can roll with the changes and carry on. We've also got a lot of value from the huge number of "things that happen this year" sections - two of those have become major plotlines for us.

      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2016 \n edited November 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Last night, despite being depressed about election results, I ran my open-table, West Marches-style, social sandbox: City of Brass (D&D 5e). I had five players, only two of whom had played CoB before. Two were relatively new to gaming. They all seemed to have a lot of fun.

      They confronted and almost murdered Dorrim, the pimp who had moved into the commune. He was taking advantage of Hengritte, who was funding her addiction to spirit potions through prostitution.

      They lured him to a room and shoved him down a trap door into the basement, where he had a hard fall. They were gonna kill him, but then had a crisis of conscience and let him out.

      With no other plan than "let him out," he did turn on them. He left peacefully but returned the next day with some strong arms from the Brass Clubs. There was a bit of a scuffle in the commune, but one of the PCs lured him away from his guards and used shocking grasp on him, killing him. They convinced a few of the Brass Clubs to leave but seem to be on the lam, hiding from the rest.

      ETA:
      It turns out it was exactly what I needed. PCs with agency fixing their social problems. Young, liberal players laughing and having fun and not thinking about real life for a bit.
      \n

      \n\nJeff_B_Slater \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2016 \n edited November 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n We played Risus, while it was fun we weren't convicted that the dice rules didn't have issues. The way dice are removed kind of inevitability ends up in a short of determanistic death spiral for a single player (in the sense that as you lose more dice the probability of overcoming continues to diminish); the only answer seems to be to group up, which tends to lead to a death spiral for the enemy. This was the consensus of the group, maybe we missed something. We did although have a fun time
      \n

      \n\nRafu \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Our game of Freebooting Venus had to be put on indefinite hiatus - frozen in place - because of real life reasons, as it happens.

      To fill that void, we started a three-player eerie supernatural horror game set in 18th century France, in a small country village. There are philosophes on the run, scheming abbey monks, a murder mystery and an old ruined church rumored to be haunted. We're using Archipelago third edition rules, although there are a lot of emergent similarities with Okult in how the game's shaping up.
      \n

      \n\nAviatrix \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n A few things happened this week that interrupted my after action report, so I barely remember Metatopia: but the highlights...

      The King Is Dead: Vincent Baker's "storytelling via minigame" was a really interesting take on story games; I enjoyed playing one of movers of a crumbling kingdom, in my case backed by the Untamed Tribes. An' when the boyos gi goin', 'tis hard ta stop 'em...

      Pasion de los Pasiones, the vaguely powered by the apocalypse game of Spanish language Telenovelas. Fantastic fun for everyone, and I got to play the evil twin of my old foil Danielle :-)

      And back home:

      The Unanswered Question, aka "Tatters of the King in '50s America": We said goodbye to a beloved minor character at a rally in Golden Gate Park. At a wild avant garde party a new drug made the rounds; the Investigators got to finally see the full version of "The Unanswered Question, or The Stranger and the Queen", the based-on-the-King in Yellow television film they had only seen part of previously. Jimmy took some revenge, and an NPC was revealed to be an old friend...sort of.

      Good fun and a nice antidote to my general mood.
      \n

      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2016 \n edited November 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I hosted an all-weekend gaming event at my house and played four different games.

      I ran Apocalypse World. There was a Hocus running a sort of video-watching cult, an Operator trying to help with the food scarcity, and a Battle Babe kicking asses when needed. A bad Hocus move threw the cult into schism and they spent their time trying to tamp down rebellion while dealmaking with the local Raver gang, who wanted to be "entertained" (with a big video show) in exchange for food and batteries.

      I facilitated The Shab-al-Hiri Roach. I played a weaselly entomologist with passions for deception and manipulation. It all ended with the burning of the Gamma Gamma Gamma house and everyone's death. I got roached, then unroached (killing my deception passion), and then in the final event, roached again.

      I ran D&D 5e (my City of Brass) for a couple hours. The comrades cleaned up the pimp's dead body, left for someone else to deal with by the last group who played. They tried to help Hengritte kick her habit, but learned that there might actually be something cool going on when she takes spirit potions.

      I ran In a Wicked Age using the Death-Kings of War oracle (IIRC). The PCs were a genius trapped in a mirror, a sand-and-wind demon seeking return of the gold ring that had been ripped from his nose, and a mystic sage martial artist trying to forge a grand magic item. The NPCs included a petty-wizard, an enemy champion, and the queen's sorcerer.

      On Saturday, we had 9-10 people, so there was another table. John D. ran Blades in the Dark. I can't remember if there was a second table for the Saturday early session, or what they played.

      It was a good weekend.
      \n

      \n To celebrate my kickstarter being almost done, I hunkered down and played Follow with the old Kingdom dream team. And it gave me a headache. An actual headache, but the best possible kind, since it was a headache from laughing too hard for too long. The human body can only take so much.

      Our Heist: kids stealing candy from the mysterious Wonka-like Blum Candy Factory. But oh so serious with our tiny schemes and tiny betrayals and tiny love triangles.

      "I just needed enough candy to get out from under, to start a new life\u2026 to get clean again."

      It was Ocean's 10 going on 11.

      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n

      \n
      \n
      \n Is it cheesy to say "long time reader, first time writer" on a forum these days? Anyway, I got to play a lot of fun stuff this week!

      I played MF0: Firebrands for the first time this past weekend. So many dangerous mech fights, so many romantic gazes! I'd like to play it again now that I've got a hang of the various prompts. I have difficulty parsing a large amount of information all at once, so a second shot (and a longer one if possible) is definitely in order. With many, many dances hopefully!

      Then it was the aforementioned Oceans 10 Going On 11 Follow game. So many amazing moments--"Now that you're in charge, have some big chew..." "No, I can't. I can't work under the influence."

      And last night at Story Games Olympia I facilitated Shooting the Moon! I hadn't played it for quite a few years so it was kind of stop and go, especially when we came to the dice stuff. I remembered how much I enjoyed character set-up (we did the SGS character-blind set-up). We were the witch resistance trying to overthrow the government in the ancient world! It was excellent.

      \n\nmilesgaborit \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I got to play so many brilliant things in the last week!

      Last Thursday I played session 2 of my newish Burning Wheel game about abortionists and aristocrats, which went smashingly. The player of the abortionist character is new to RPGs, but nonetheless nearly pushed her way into a 3 way duel of wits because she is bringing the heat! The player of the aristocrat character got seduced by the object of his erstwhile romantic pursuits...afterward, he had the impression that things had gone too easily for him, and he's right (though he won't find out how bad he screwed up until next session).

      Over the weekend I had a whole mess of amazing people over to my place for a little house con action. Got to play two of Mr. Morningstar's LARPs - Juggernaut and The Climb. I'd played The Climb a few times before, but this was the first time I got to go to the summit and do the 'radio play' portion of the game...and it was amazing! Juggernaut was fabulous, and totally created a convincing sense of dread and pre-destination...feeling powerless has never been so fun and intense! (It helped that the groups I played those games with were composed of 100% elite game peeps)

      House con also gave me a chance to play Bluebeard's Bride. I really enjoyed the inter-character sniping in that game, and am super keen to GM it. The parts I like best when I GM horror are describing creepy, enigmatic, otherworldly things...and that's like pretty much all you do as a GM in BB. No set up, no building a sense of normalcy to contrast the horror - you just bring the creepy!

      Finally, this Wednesday I played in the 2nd session of a game of The Warren that one of the 12 year olds in my weekly kids group is running. It's a big group, so the fact that the kid running it was able to give everybody spotlight and manage the group splitting into three was pretty impressive. He needs to learn to drop the hammer when it's time to make a hard move, but a lot of folks have trouble getting comfortable with that aspect of PbtA - really looking forward to seeing how this little duder develops as a GM!
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nRafu \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Saturday evening game: first session (out of two at least, possibly more) in another playtest run of The Truth Dies with Them, my cold-case police procedural game about capital punishment. I have no idea when I'll manage to get the rules down on paper as an actual, usable text, you know - for the time being, I'm content enough playing the game and confirming it works more-or-less as intended.

      Sunday afternoon game: second session in our Archipelago III 1700s France small town supernatural horror/murder mystery. The general feeling still reminds us of a game of Okult a lot, but in addition to that the details of the supernatural mystery are taking up weird Left Coast-ish colors (is the consciousness of people from the past actually possessing those who venture into the swamp? or is there an alternate explanation to their amnesia?) while the murder mystery side of the tale has just gone for a political spin (has some higher Church authority ordered the abbot dead just to keep him from investigating the mysteries surrounding the old ruined church building?).
      \n

      \n\nLisa Padol \n\n\n

      \n\nNovember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I played a lot of stuff at Metatopia, most of which involved R-maps that got me right into the game, PbtA, or both.

      The Wednesday after, I did character, homeroom, and town generation for Monsterhearts 2, with a couple of folks I usually game with and a couple of folks who had been looking for games. (That reminds me that I need to calendar the next session.)

      This past Wednesday, I did Kerberos Machinery of Life, adapting a Victoriana scenario which is 90-95% solid, and 5-10% WTF (involving a scheme a friend pronounced "underpants gnomes" level of WTF, in fact), counting on the way we play to keep me from having to figure out that last 5-10%. This worked. I now have some ideas for what to swap in, along with notes for what leads NPCs will be coming back to PCs with, who Dorian's seducing now, and the kinds of trouble Dr. Alexander's family can get itself into.

      \n\nCaroline_Hobbs \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I tried a couple of games I'd never played before this week!

      My first was A Scoundrel in the Deep, which is from issue 6 of Worlds Without Master. When the facilitator was pitching it, I couldn't help but think of Earthsea, so that's what we did. As the Scoundrel I very stressfully navigated my way out of the caves and into redemption, confronted at every turn by shadows from my past misdeeds. In the game you have until a match burns out in your hand to solve a problem by the light of the flame. I only burnt my fingers once! The rules are written a little opaquely and not brief enough to be read by candlelight. But don't be fooled--it's a really great game. Will definitely play again.

      My other game this week was Bootstrapping, which is a game in fresh playtest by Max Hervieux. It's set up as a series of mini-games, with each game being presented very nicely on small cards. One player is the new sentient AI, coming to terms with its identity and future. The other player is various humans, depending on the mini-game. The game has some interesting role-playing constraints and I'm excited to try it out further as it develops!
      \n

      \n\nDecember 2016 \n

      \n
      \nFall of Magic a two-shot at our Tuesday night meetup. Ellamura the apprentice raged against the death of magic and the unfairness of the magus to let this happen; Fox the Fox of Mistwood pondered the existential nature of being a fox who speaks; Justice the golem preserved the memories of all the other golems; the scholar of Istallia recorded all that happened, and the heir of Stormguard prayed that he would be a better man than his father. As always, good fun.

      Thou Art But a Warrior we only got a taste of it, but it was a lot of fun. Gibreel the lawyer competed with his rival, an imam, for the love of a beautiful lady; Ikram killed an emissary of King Alfonso; and the mother of another knight was kidnapped by the Christians. Just another day in old al-Andalus.

      The Unanswered Question The group were transported to Carcosa, here a bustling art deco metropolis. Jimmy met an old friend who wore his face; Charleston got paid back by the Dark Lord; Candace saw the date of her death hanging under her portrait in the Museum of Carcosa; and Temperance found herself questioning her faith after almost falling into the hands of the living god. Oh, and they may have allowed Cassilda to escape to Earth. A nice winter finale and the end of Act One (Act Two is going to be a bunch of purist adventures with a "roadtrip across America" frame.)
      \n

      \n I'm not sure about that. None of the PCs has yet initiated or been the victim of physical violence. This is atypical of my experience with the game. Some of this is because one time I thought it would happen, the PC found a way to shut down the NPC in a way that made sense to us, and the player rolled very, very well. Note: I am not counting the wounded, possibly dead guy who was found, not for these purposes.

      \n\nRafu \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Technically, last week.

      On Thursday, continued playing The Truth Dies With Them: we now have a pretty messy case with lots of potential alternate murderers who have their motives - lots of dark secrets in the family - but still haven't found that one piece of evidence that changes it all. Meanwhile, I'm convinced I have a more-or-less solid ruleset and should definitely sketch out a public playtest document or something.

      Sunday, one more installment in our 1700s France small-town horror Archipelago. Things have definitely escalated with the arrival of the bishop in town - two out of three PCs are now fugitives and somebody has set fire to the abbey library (was it the bishop?). Ghosts from the past are getting more and more talkative. The destiny points we wrote this session are to be our last ones, definitely. We need closure and denouement now, which won't be too easy, considering we've had supernatural events on stage with no agreement between players as to what's actually up (quite a challenging playstyle).

      I really hope to finish both games next weekend.

      \n\nDreamer \n\n\n

      \n\nDecember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n An ongoing solo session of Trollbabe. I've been using some #WritingWithDice, but mostly experimenting with some automated tools like the iOS predictive text app CreativeWriter, and a JavaScript oracle. The combination is the closest I've felt to not having much control over what would otherwise be GM output.
      \n

      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Ran City of Brass for my Pathfinder group (we usually play Pathfinder and Marc GMs; some players were out so I offered to run D&D 5e instead).

      All the usual things.

      In addition, Marc's husband Craig played. He never plays! Craig focused his character's time on befriending the women in the neighborhood laundry service, earning their trust, and learning the scuttlebutt of the city from them.

      I kept thinking, "If that's how you want to spend your time, suuuure," and worried a lot that he was not having fun.

      At the end, he praised the game, said it was refreshingly different than other D&D games he's played in, and specifically called out that 1) it was about social matters, 2) it wasn't all about combat, and 3) choices had consequences.

      \n
      \n I played a late-beta run of my pick-up-and-play card-based game Three Dooms. It's about a stone age town and generating its history as you deal with different events.

      It went really well! Except for our town's 66th year, that one was bad.

      The coolest story to come out was a mystery cult that started early on, centered on the crystals and geodes we mined. The cult developed and changed in really interesting ways as we played, gradually becoming less secret and more accepted.

      \n\nLisa Padol \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I played Graf Linus von Schuluz in Diaspora, a man who (falsely) claimed credit for an airstrike on a town on Old Terra (where he lives) (because it was necessary to undercut the faction that actually did it) and nuked his own family's castle (which really was necessary, but sadly insufficient to permanently halt a godlike being), and who is trying to keep up with what is happening (some kind of portal in the center of the moon -- Old Terra's moon -- the one that the folks from Galliard didn't build. At least, Linus thinks it was them, but there are currently pirates from Hammerhead working for the Galliardans there, which is a nuisance because Linus can't outbid Galliard). Still, despite everything, Old Terra has not gone up in flames, so that's... probably something.

      I ran Kerberos II, Machinery of Life, using a Victoriana scenario, as usual. And, I figured we'd wrap that particular scenario tonight. Right, the fight with the vampire, a minor thing, surely, and then information leading to the big thing, which I actually mapped out to make sure I had an explanation that a) worked for my game (which isn't Victoriana) and b) made a whole lot more sense to me than the explanation in the scenario (which, apart from that bit is solid, but that bit -- well, a friend of mine described the scene as on the level of the underpants gnomes).

      So, things took a left turn at the vampire. Dorian Gray wanted to see how she fed, but his companions kept attacking her! And he was crap at stopping them. And then, he addressed her, saying, "My dear, shall we continue this conversation at the Kerberos Club?"

      This is an NPC intended as a minor or moderate nuisance in Victoriana, you understand, pretty much supposed to be disposed of by the PCs. But, this _is_ Kerberos Club, not Victoriana, and the club's let in far stranger and more lethal things than vampires. Sure, this left some fall out to be dealt with, but the players and PCs are an imaginative lot.

      Of course, we're currently using Atomic Robo Fate, and there are some moving parts of the system we've yet to use. Organizational Resources. Scientifick Hypotheses! And collateral damage on the game world.

      This last may finally see some use. Our clockwork lady pretended to be a wheelbarrow and then transformed into her best interpretation of a horseless carriage (she'd seen the blueprints of the prototype in progress briefly, so how hard could it be, right?) and then modified it so that she was driving herself, and then drove through the streets of 1860 Kerberean London at night as the very item whose building laborers are protesting against. And various revolutionary factions had a falling out leading to an apparent shooting of a member of the gentry, who was unharmed, but this is still going to be A Thing. And the factory where the horseless carriage prototype is supposed to be built got a bit damaged in the fight with the vampire, which is, oddly, the least of anyone's problems.

      And Dr. Alexander is delighted to have a vampire and a rehabilitated victim of the vampire (likely de facto some flavor of dhampire) to study. They have a schedule of appointments with him and instructions about what sort of journals about their biology to keep.
      \n

      \n\nRafu \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nThe Truth Dies with Them: best possible ending, in that we managed to save the prisoner's life - through an unexpectedly lucky draw when we were about to run out of time - and it turned out he was innocent indeed. His sister (well, half-sister, as it turned out) was the actual murderer, but what will happen of her is out of the scope of this game... Three out of four detectives either went back to their happy everyday life or found a way to - perhaps - fix it and make it happy again. One still has huge unresolved issues to deal with - his personal investment in the case having proved a complete red herring and dead end.

      1700s Archipelago: we also played this game to an ending, and quite a satisfactory one. All of the main characters ended up as fugitives, for different reasons, but also with the moral upper hand over their enemies. The old haunted church finally sunk into the swamp for good, the dead more or less laid to rest, and the blood-stained Nail of the Cross is lost to all - but their story lives on to be told and re-told by itinerant actors.

      \n A couple of online playtests of my 'Create a science fiction story' mini-game for the new version of Left Coast.

      \n\nAdam_Dray \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I played a 1st level paladin in a Pathfinder game. It was a two-hour one-shot. It was awful. The GM had skills, but his scenario was a railroad (and he knew it).

      Not knowing anything about the scenario we'd be playing, I selected the paladin from a pile of 10-15 pregens. He had three skills, none of which turned out to be useful in the adventure. He had no spells. He had scale armor, a sword, and a crossbow, most of which turned out not to be useful in the adventure.

      The other characters were a monk and a cleric. We explored a swamp, searching for the remains of another paladin who had died out here. We collectively decided we had good motives for finding the remains (religiously-oriented motivations, not murder hobo ones).

      First we found a body in the swamp and a ghost appeared and told us where to find the adventure: around a huge tree down river. We explored a bit, found a boat, and took it down river toward the tree. The river became impassable in a swamp.

      The next big encounter was with some kind of waterskipping eel hound. The GM told us that we knew that they usually traveled in packs and this lone monster was running toward his buddies. If he got there, we'd have huge trouble. He launched into a set of minigame rules that abstractly measured our distance to the eel hound. The cleric and I quickly realized that the halfling monk was super well suited to the game's challenges, while we were not. The two of us comically flailed around in the swamp, making decisions like "stand still and shoot, sprint across this flat, or try to cut it off by crossing the dangerous area." The monk ended up catching up to it and killing it. We were 2-3 steps behind it the whole time.

      We found a giant skull that was the black dragon that the now-deceased NPC party had killed.

      Eventually we slogged to the tree, found a pool behind it, saw a glowing body at the bottom of the pool, talked to the dead paladin's ghost and learned there was a baby troll guarding the pool. We pushed a giant boulder into the pool to try to lure it out. It came out. We shot at it. I decided I'd be best used as a tank while they shot at it, so I ran to meet it, but misunderstood the risk. I had to make an Athletics or Acrobatics check to get across some slippery tree trunks. Having neither, I flubbed my roll and slipped into the pool 30 feet below.

      Wearing scale armor, I had to succeed at DC 10 Swim checks to avoid taking drowning damage. A DC 15 success would let me swim to the edge instead of treading water in place. I made the first check to tread water. I failed the second and took drowning damage but didn't die. I made the third to tread water. By that time, the troll was dead and my party helped me out.

      None of it was fun for me. All of my choices felt forced. At the end, the GM and one of the players talked about how much of a railroad it is, and the player said, "But in such a short time, you really have no choice." I openly disagreed with him, since I run a D&D sandbox adventure pretty much every other week at the same venue.
      \n

      \n\nLisa Padol \n\n\n

      \n\nDecember 2016 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n I ran Monsterhearts again. We're using the sneak peak rules from v. 2 with v. 1 for everything that doesn't cover. One thing I can't tell is if v. 2 is supposed to use highlighting. We're assuming not, and it does have the "mark xp on a failure" rule.

      I'm needing to remember I have one player whose PC will play it safe every time (at least thus far) (mostly(*)) and one whose PC lacks normal common sense (raised by wolves). Any normal teenager would climb out the window to go to The Party. She walked out the front door.

      (*) His player did point out to me that a) the PC used a hex and b) rolled in the 7-9 range which c) means that the hex is not automatically "easily reversible". Oh yes, the hex means the hexee (who was already accusing the PC of devil worship and the like) is seeing demons everywhere. So, you understand, "play it safe" doesn't mean bad play for Monsterhearts, just that I can't make assumptions.

      \n\nRafu \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nDecember 2016 \n edited December 2016
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n Played 2-player Lovecraftesque on planes and trains while traveling. Card-less variant, because on planes and trains. It was a good game - better than what I expected, actually. I had picked it mostly for the form factor (something two people could play on a plane or train) but was afraid the game was too focused on celebrating its source material by reenacting a formula - much to my delight, I was proven wrong. There was just enough wiggle space for us to make the story something our own, with a main character we felt for in a world we cared about. And the Final Horror was genuinely scary not just to the other player as the Witness, but to me as the Narrator as well.

      \n\nMike_Sands \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nJanuary 2017 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \nTales From The Loop, an improvised one-shot to assess it for games on demand at Kapcon (Wellington, NZ) which is coming up in two weeks.

      It was really fun. I riffed off the character generation questions (that include two 'weird things you noticed recently' in particular) and the story twisted and turned as the dice came out. The story worked (there were some loose threads left, but the main mystery and problem got solved). Also, I got to include some other-dimensional killer axebeaks!
